<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="Gold medal – first place" data-file-height="16" data-file-width="16" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gold_medal_icon_%28G_initial%29.svg/16px-Gold_medal_icon_%28G_initial%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gold_medal_icon_%28G_initial%29.svg/24px-Gold_medal_icon_%28G_initial%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gold_medal_icon_%28G_initial%29.svg/32px-Gold_medal_icon_%28G_initial%29.svg.png 2x" title="Gold medal – first place" width="16"/></td>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/1956_Summer_Olympics" title="1956 Summer Olympics">1956 Melbourne</a></span></td>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;"><a href="/wiki/1956_United_States_men%27s_Olympic_basketball_team" title="1956 United States men's Olympic basketball team">Team competition</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>William Felton</b> "<b>Bill</b>" <b>Russell</b> (born February 12, 1934) is an American retired professional <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a> player. Russell played <a href="/wiki/Center_(basketball)" title="Center (basketball)">center</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Celtics" title="Boston Celtics">Boston Celtics</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a> (NBA) from 1956 to 1969. A five-time <a href="/wiki/NBA_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="NBA Most Valuable Player Award">NBA Most Valuable Player</a> and a twelve-time <a href="/wiki/NBA_All-Star_Game" title="NBA All-Star Game">All-Star</a>, he was the centerpiece of the Celtics <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynasty_(sports)" title="Dynasty (sports)">dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_NBA_players_with_most_championships" title="List of NBA players with most championships">winning eleven NBA championships</a> during his thirteen-year career. Along with <a href="/wiki/Henri_Richard" title="Henri Richard">Henri Richard</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a>, Russell holds the record for the most championships won by an athlete in a North American sports league. Before his professional career, Russell led the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Dons_men%27s_basketball" title="San Francisco Dons men's basketball">University of San Francisco</a> to two consecutive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Division I Basketball Championship">NCAA championships</a> (1955, 1956). He also won a gold medal at the <a href="/wiki/Basketball_at_the_1956_Summer_Olympics" title="Basketball at the 1956 Summer Olympics">1956 Summer Olympics</a> as captain of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_men%27s_national_basketball_team" title="United States men's national basketball team">U.S. national basketball team</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Russell is widely considered one of the best players in NBA history. He was listed as between 6 ft 9 in (2.06 m) and 6 ft 10 in (2.08 m), and his <a href="/wiki/Block_(basketball)" title="Block (basketball)">shot-blocking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Man-to-man_defense" title="Man-to-man defense">man-to-man defense</a> were major reasons for the Celtics' success. He also inspired his teammates to elevate their own defensive play. Russell was equally notable for his <a href="/wiki/Rebound_(basketball)" title="Rebound (basketball)">rebounding</a> abilities. He <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_annual_rebounding_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association annual rebounding leaders">led the NBA in rebounds</a> four times, had a dozen consecutive seasons of 1,000 or more rebounds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and remains second all-time in both <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_career_rebounding_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association career rebounding leaders">total rebounds and rebounds per game</a>. He is one of just two NBA players (the other being prominent rival <a href="/wiki/Wilt_Chamberlain" title="Wilt Chamberlain">Wilt Chamberlain</a>) to have grabbed more than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_players_with_most_rebounds_in_a_game" title="List of National Basketball Association players with most rebounds in a game">50 rebounds in a game</a>. Though never the focal point of the Celtics' <a href="/wiki/Offense_(sports)" title="Offense (sports)">offense</a>, Russell also scored 14,522 career <a href="/wiki/Point_(basketball)" title="Point (basketball)">points</a> and provided effective passing.</p>
<p>Playing in the wake of pioneers like <a href="/wiki/Earl_Lloyd" title="Earl Lloyd">Earl Lloyd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Cooper_(basketball)" title="Chuck Cooper (basketball)">Chuck Cooper</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweetwater_Clifton" title="Sweetwater Clifton">Sweetwater Clifton</a>, Russell was the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> player to achieve superstar status in the NBA. He also served a three-season (1966–69) stint as <a href="/wiki/Player-coach" title="Player-coach">player-coach</a> for the Celtics, becoming the first African American NBA coach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> For his accomplishments in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a> on and off the court, <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> awarded him the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> in 2011.</p>
<p>Russell is one of only seven players in history to win an NCAA Championship, an NBA Championship, and an Olympic gold medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> He was inducted into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="Basketball Hall of Fame">Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="National Collegiate Basketball Hall of Fame">National Collegiate Basketball Hall of Fame</a>. He was selected into the <a href="/wiki/NBA_25th_Anniversary_Team" title="NBA 25th Anniversary Team">NBA 25th Anniversary Team</a> in 1971 and the <a href="/wiki/NBA_35th_Anniversary_Team" title="NBA 35th Anniversary Team">NBA 35th Anniversary Team</a> in 1980, and named as one of the <a href="/wiki/50_Greatest_Players_in_NBA_History" title="50 Greatest Players in NBA History">50 Greatest Players in NBA History</a> in 1996, one of only four players to receive all three honors. In 2007, he was enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/FIBA_Hall_of_Fame" title="FIBA Hall of Fame">FIBA Hall of Fame</a>. In 2009, the NBA announced that the <a href="/wiki/NBA_Finals" title="NBA Finals">NBA Finals</a> Most Valuable Player trophy would be named the <a href="/wiki/Bill_Russell_NBA_Finals_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Bill Russell NBA Finals Most Valuable Player Award">Bill Russell NBA Finals Most Valuable Player Award</a> in honor of Russell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalsmvp_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-finalsmvp-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bill Russell was born to Charles Russell and Katie Russell in <a href="/wiki/West_Monroe,_Louisiana" title="West Monroe, Louisiana">West Monroe, Louisiana</a>. Like almost all southern towns and cities of that time, West Monroe was a highly segregated place, and the Russells often struggled with racism in their daily lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Current_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Current-5">[5]</a></sup> Once, Russell's father was refused service at a gas station until the staff had taken care of all the white customers. When his father attempted to leave and find a different station, the attendant stuck a shotgun in his face and threatened to kill him if he didn't stay and wait his turn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Current_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Current-5">[5]</a></sup> At another time, Russell's mother was walking outside in a fancy dress when a policeman accosted her. He told her to go home and remove the dress, which he described as "white woman's clothing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Current_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Current-5">[5]</a></sup> Because <a href="/wiki/Second_Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Second Great Migration (African American)">large numbers of blacks were moving to the West during World War II</a> to look for work there, Russell's father moved the family out of Louisiana when Russell was eight years old and settled them in <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Current_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Current-5">[5]</a></sup> While there the family fell into poverty, and Russell spent his childhood living in a series of <a href="/wiki/Public_housing#United_States" title="Public housing">public housing projects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Current_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Current-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Charles Russell is described as a "stern, hard man" who was initially a janitor in a paper factory (a typical low paid, intellectually unchallenging "Negro Job", as sports journalist John Taylor commented),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor52_56_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor52_56-6">[6]</a></sup> but later became a trucker when World War II broke out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor52_56_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor52_56-6">[6]</a></sup> Being closer to his mother Katie than to his father,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor52_56_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor52_56-6">[6]</a></sup> Russell received a major emotional blow when she suddenly died when he was 12. His father gave up his trucking job and became a steel worker to be closer to his semi-orphaned children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor52_56_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-taylor52_56-6">[6]</a></sup> Russell has stated that his father became his childhood hero, later followed up by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minneapolis_Lakers" title="Minneapolis Lakers">Minneapolis Lakers</a> superstar <a href="/wiki/George_Mikan" title="George Mikan">George "Mr. Basketball" Mikan</a>, who he met when he was in high school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chat_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-chat-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his early years, Russell struggled to develop his skills as a basketball player. Although Russell was a good runner and jumper and had large hands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor52_56_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-taylor52_56-6">[6]</a></sup> he simply did not understand the game and was cut from the team in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junior_high_school" title="Junior high school">junior high school</a>. As a <a href="/wiki/Freshman" title="Freshman">freshman</a> at <a href="/wiki/McClymonds_High_School" title="McClymonds High School">McClymonds High School</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland, California</a>, Russell was almost cut again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HS_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-HS-9">[9]</a></sup> However, coach George Powles saw Russell's raw athletic potential and encouraged him to work on his fundamentals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor52_56_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-taylor52_56-6">[6]</a></sup> Since Russell's previous experiences with white authority figures were often negative, he was delighted to receive warm words from his white coach. He worked hard and used the benefits of a growth spurt to become a decent basketball player, but it was not until his <a href="/wiki/Eleventh_grade" title="Eleventh grade">junior</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_grade" title="Twelfth grade">senior</a> years that he began to excel, winning back to back high school state championships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HS_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-HS-9">[9]</a></sup> Russell soon became noted for his unusual style of defense. He later recalled, "To play good defense ... it was told back then that you had to stay flatfooted at all times to react quickly. When I started to jump to make defensive plays and to <a href="/wiki/Block_(basketball)" title="Block (basketball)">block shots</a>, I was initially corrected, but I stuck with it, and it paid off."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-five_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-five-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of Russell's high school basketball teammates was future <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame and Museum">Baseball Hall-of-Famer</a> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Robinson" title="Frank Robinson">Frank Robinson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="College_career">College career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: College career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Russell was ignored by college recruiters and did not receive a single letter of interest until recruiter Hal DeJulio from the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Dons_men%27s_basketball" title="San Francisco Dons men's basketball">University of San Francisco</a> (USF) watched him in a high school game. DeJulio was not impressed by Russell's meager scoring and "atrocious fundamentals",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor50_51_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor50_51-12">[12]</a></sup> but sensed that the young <a href="/wiki/Center_(basketball)" title="Center (basketball)">center</a> had an extraordinary instinct for the game, especially in the clutch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor50_51_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor50_51-12">[12]</a></sup> When DeJulio offered Russell a scholarship, the latter eagerly accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HS_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-HS-9">[9]</a></sup> Sports journalist John Taylor described it as a watershed in Russell's life, because Russell realized that basketball was his one chance to escape poverty and racism; as a consequence, Russell swore to make the best of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor52_56_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-taylor52_56-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bill_russell_usf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="760" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bill_russell_usf.jpg/180px-Bill_russell_usf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bill_russell_usf.jpg/270px-Bill_russell_usf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bill_russell_usf.jpg/360px-Bill_russell_usf.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bill_russell_usf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Russell with the USF uniform c 1953–56</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At USF, Russell became the new starting center for coach <a href="/wiki/Phil_Woolpert" title="Phil Woolpert">Phil Woolpert</a>. Woolpert emphasized defense and deliberate half-court play, concepts that favored defensive standout Russell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor57_67_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor57_67-13">[13]</a></sup> Woolpert's choices of how to deploy his players were unaffected by issues of skin color. In 1954, he became the first coach of a major college basketball squad to start three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> players: Russell, <a href="/wiki/K._C._Jones" title="K. C. Jones">K. C. Jones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hal_Perry_(basketball)" title="Hal Perry (basketball)">Hal Perry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usf_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-usf-14">[14]</a></sup> In his USF years, Russell used his relative lack of bulk to develop a unique style of defense: instead of purely guarding the opposing center, he used his quickness and speed to play help defense against opposing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forward_(basketball)" title="Forward (basketball)">forwards</a> and aggressively challenge their shots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor57_67_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor57_67-13">[13]</a></sup> Combining the stature and shot-blocking skills of a center with the foot speed of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guard_(basketball)" title="Guard (basketball)">guard</a>, Russell became the centerpiece of a USF team that soon became a force in college basketball. After USF kept <a href="/wiki/Holy_Cross_Crusaders_men%27s_basketball" title="Holy Cross Crusaders men's basketball">Holy Cross</a> star <a href="/wiki/Tom_Heinsohn" title="Tom Heinsohn">Tom Heinsohn</a> scoreless in an entire half, <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i> wrote, "If [Russell] ever learns to hit the basket, they're going to have to rewrite the rules."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor57_67_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor57_67-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bill_Russell_University_of_San_Francisco.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="789" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bill_Russell_University_of_San_Francisco.jpeg/160px-Bill_Russell_University_of_San_Francisco.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bill_Russell_University_of_San_Francisco.jpeg/240px-Bill_Russell_University_of_San_Francisco.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bill_Russell_University_of_San_Francisco.jpeg/320px-Bill_Russell_University_of_San_Francisco.jpeg 2x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bill_Russell_University_of_San_Francisco.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Russell, circa 1954–56</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, the games were often difficult for the USF squad. Russell and his African American teammates became targets of racist jeers, particularly on the road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jwr_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-jwr-15">[15]</a></sup> In one notable incident, hotels in <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City" title="Oklahoma City">Oklahoma City</a> refused to admit Russell and his black teammates while they were in town for the 1954 All-College Tournament. In protest, the whole team decided to camp out in a closed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_dorm" title="College dorm">college dorm</a>, which was later called an important bonding experience for the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usf_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-usf-14">[14]</a></sup> Decades later, Russell explained that his experiences hardened him against abuse of all kinds. "I never permitted myself to be a victim", he said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ConvSI_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-ConvSI-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ConvUSA_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-ConvUSA-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Racism also shaped his lifelong paradigm as a team player. "At that time", he has said, "it was never acceptable that a black player was the best. That did not happen ... My junior year in college, I had what I thought was the one of the best college seasons ever. We won 28 out of 29 games. We won the National Championship. I was the [Most Valuable Player] at the Final Four. I was first team All American. I averaged over 20 <a href="/wiki/Point_(basketball)" title="Point (basketball)">points</a> and over 20 <a href="/wiki/Rebound_(basketball)" title="Rebound (basketball)">rebounds</a>, and I was the only guy in college blocking shots. So after the season was over, they had a Northern California banquet, and they picked another center as Player of the Year in Northern California. Well, that let me know that if I were to accept these as the final judges of my career I would die a bitter old man." So he made a conscious decision, he said, to put the team first and foremost, and not worry about individual achievements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the hardwood, his experiences were far more pleasant. Russell led USF to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Division I Basketball Championship">NCAA championships</a> in 1955 and 1956, including a string of 55 consecutive victories. He became known for his strong defense and shot-blocking skills, once denying 13 shots in a game. <a href="/wiki/UCLA_Bruins_men%27s_basketball" title="UCLA Bruins men's basketball">UCLA</a> coach <a href="/wiki/John_Wooden" title="John Wooden">John Wooden</a> called Russell "the greatest defensive man I've ever seen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usf_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-usf-14">[14]</a></sup> During his college career, Russell averaged 20.7 <a href="/wiki/Points_per_game" title="Points per game">points per game</a> and 20.3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rebounds_per_game" title="Rebounds per game">rebounds per game</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> Besides basketball, Russell represented USF in <a href="/wiki/Track_and_field" title="Track and field">track and field</a> events. He competed in the 440 yards (400 m) race, which he could complete in 49.6 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> He also participated in the <a href="/wiki/High_jump" title="High jump">high jump</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/Track_%26_Field_News" title="Track &amp; Field News">Track &amp; Field News</a></i> ranked him as the seventh-best high jumper in the world in 1956. That year, Russell won high jump titles at the Central California <a href="/wiki/Amateur_Athletic_Union" title="Amateur Athletic Union">AAU</a> meet, the Pacific AAU meet, and the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Relays" title="West Coast Relays">West Coast Relays</a>. One of his highest jumps occurred at the West Coast Relays, where he achieved a mark of 6 feet <span class="frac nowrap">9<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> inches (2.064 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>After his years at USF, the <a href="/wiki/Harlem_Globetrotters" title="Harlem Globetrotters">Harlem Globetrotters</a> invited Russell to join their <a href="/wiki/Exhibition_game" title="Exhibition game">exhibition</a> basketball squad. Russell, who was sensitive to any racial prejudice, was enraged by the fact that owner <a href="/wiki/Abe_Saperstein" title="Abe Saperstein">Abe Saperstein</a> would only discuss the matter with Woolpert. While Saperstein spoke to Woolpert in a meeting, Globetrotters assistant coach Harry Hanna tried to entertain Russell with jokes. The USF center was livid after this snub and declined the offer: he reasoned that if Saperstein was too smart to speak with him, then he was too smart to play for Saperstein. Instead, Russell made himself eligible for the <a href="/wiki/1956_NBA_draft" title="1956 NBA draft">1956 NBA draft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor66_71_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor66_71-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1956_NBA_draft">1956 NBA draft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1956 NBA draft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the 1956 NBA draft, <a href="/wiki/Boston_Celtics" title="Boston Celtics">Boston Celtics</a> coach <a href="/wiki/Red_Auerbach" title="Red Auerbach">Red Auerbach</a> had set his sights on Russell, thinking his defensive toughness and rebounding prowess were the missing pieces the Celtics needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> In retrospect, Auerbach's thoughts were unorthodox. In that period, centers and forwards were defined by their offensive output, and their ability to play defense was secondary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, Boston's chances of getting Russell seemed slim. Because the Celtics had finished second in the previous season and the worst teams had the highest draft picks, the Celtics had slipped too low in the draft order to pick Russell. In addition, Auerbach had already used his <a href="/wiki/NBA_territorial_pick" title="NBA territorial pick">territorial pick</a> to acquire talented forward <a href="/wiki/Tom_Heinsohn" title="Tom Heinsohn">Tom Heinsohn</a>. But Auerbach knew that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rochester_Royals" title="Rochester Royals">Rochester Royals</a>, who owned the first draft pick, already had a skilled rebounder in <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Stokes" title="Maurice Stokes">Maurice Stokes</a>, were looking for an outside shooting guard and were unwilling to pay Russell the $25,000 signing bonus he requested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor67_74_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor67_74-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis_Hawks" title="St. Louis Hawks">St. Louis Hawks</a>, who owned the second pick, drafted Russell, but were vying for Celtics center <a href="/wiki/Ed_Macauley" title="Ed Macauley">Ed Macauley</a>, a six-time <a href="/wiki/NBA_All-Star_Game" title="NBA All-Star Game">All-Star</a> who had roots in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>. Auerbach agreed to trade Macauley, who had previously asked to be traded to St. Louis in order to be with his sick son, if the Hawks gave up Russell. The owner of St Louis called Auerbach later and demanded more in the trade. Not only did he want Macauley, who was the Celtics premier player at the time, he wanted <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Hagan" title="Cliff Hagan">Cliff Hagan</a>, who had been serving in the military for three years and had not yet played for the Celtics. After much debate, Auerbach agreed to give up Hagan, and the Hawks made the trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>During that same draft, Boston also drafted guard <a href="/wiki/K._C._Jones" title="K. C. Jones">K. C. Jones</a>, Russell's former USF teammate. Thus, in one night, the Celtics managed to draft three future <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="Basketball Hall of Fame">Hall of Famers</a>: Russell, Jones and Heinsohn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> The Russell draft-day trade was later called one of the most important trades in the history of North American sports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor67_74_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor67_74-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1956_Olympics">1956 Olympics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1956 Olympics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Before his NBA rookie year, Russell was the captain of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_men%27s_national_basketball_team" title="United States men's national basketball team">U.S. national basketball team</a> that competed at the <a href="/wiki/Basketball_at_the_1956_Summer_Olympics" title="Basketball at the 1956 Summer Olympics">1956 Summer Olympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, Australia. <a href="/wiki/Avery_Brundage" title="Avery Brundage">Avery Brundage</a>, head of the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a>, argued that Russell had already signed a professional contract and thus was no longer an amateur, but Russell prevailed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor67_74_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor67_74-23">[23]</a></sup> He had the option to skip the tournament and play a full season for the Celtics, but he was determined to play in the Olympics. He later commented that he would have participated in the <a href="/wiki/High_jump" title="High jump">high jump</a> if he had been snubbed by the basketball team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chat_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-chat-7">[7]</a></sup> Under coach Gerald Tucker, Russell helped the national team win the <a href="/wiki/Gold_medal" title="Gold medal">gold medal</a> in Melbourne, defeating the Soviet Union 89–55 in the final game. The United States dominated the tournament, winning by an average of 53.5 points per game. Russell led the team in scoring, averaging 14.1 points per game for the competition. His Celtics teammate K. C. Jones joined him on the Olympic squad and contributed 10.9 points per game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_career">Professional career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Professional career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1956.E2.80.9359">1956–59</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1956–59">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Russell could not join the Celtics for the <a href="/wiki/1956%E2%80%9357_NBA_season" title="1956–57 NBA season">1956–57 season</a> until December, due to his Olympic commitment. After rejoining the Celtics, Russell played 48 games, averaging 14.7 points per game and a league-high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_top_rookie_rebounding_averages" title="List of National Basketball Association top rookie rebounding averages">19.6 rebounds per game</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> During this season, the Celtics featured five future Hall-of-Famers: center Russell, forwards Heinsohn and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Ramsey_(basketball)" title="Frank Ramsey (basketball)">Frank Ramsey</a>, and guards <a href="/wiki/Bill_Sharman" title="Bill Sharman">Bill Sharman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Cousy" title="Bob Cousy">Bob Cousy</a>. (K. C. Jones did not play for the Celtics until 1958 because of military service.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Russell's first Celtics game came on December 22, 1956, against the St. Louis Hawks, led by star forward <a href="/wiki/Bob_Pettit" title="Bob Pettit">Bob Pettit</a>, who held several all-time scoring records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor74_80_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor74_80-28">[28]</a></sup> Auerbach assigned Russell to shut down St. Louis's main scorer, and the rookie impressed the Boston crowd with his man-to-man defense and shot-blocking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor74_80_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor74_80-28">[28]</a></sup> In previous years, the Celtics had been a high-scoring team, but lacked the defensive presence needed to close out tight games. However, with the added defensive presence of Russell, the Celtics had laid the foundation for a dynasty. The team utilized a strong defensive approach to the game, forcing opposing teams to commit many <a href="/wiki/Turnover_(basketball)" title="Turnover (basketball)">turnovers</a>, which led to many easy <a href="/wiki/Fast_break" title="Fast break">fast break</a> points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor74_80_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor74_80-28">[28]</a></sup> Russell was an elite help defender who allowed the Celtics to play the so-called "Hey, Bill" defense: whenever a Celtic requested additional defensive help, he would shout "Hey, Bill!" Russell was so quick that he could run over for a quick <a href="/wiki/Double_team" title="Double team">double team</a> and make it back in time if the opponents tried to find the open man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor74_80_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-taylor74_80-28">[28]</a></sup> He also became famous for his shot-blocking skills: pundits called his blocks "Wilsonburgers", referring to the <a href="/wiki/Wilson_Sporting_Goods" title="Wilson Sporting Goods">Wilson</a> NBA basketballs he "shoved back into the faces of opposing shooters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor74_80_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-taylor74_80-28">[28]</a></sup> This skill also allowed the other Celtics to play their men aggressively: if they were beaten, they knew that Russell was guarding the basket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor74_80_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-taylor74_80-28">[28]</a></sup> This approach allowed the Celtics to finish with a 44–28 regular season record, the team's second-best record since beginning play in the <a href="/wiki/1946%E2%80%9347_BAA_season" title="1946–47 BAA season">1946–47 season</a>, and guaranteed a post-season appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brceltics_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-brceltics-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, Russell also received negative attention. Constantly provoked by <a href="/wiki/New_York_Knicks" title="New York Knicks">New York Knicks</a> center <a href="/wiki/Ray_Felix" title="Ray Felix">Ray Felix</a> during a game, he complained to coach Auerbach. The latter told him to take matters into his own hands, so after the next provocation, Russell punched Felix unconscious, paid a $25 fine and was no longer a target of cheap fouls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor74_80_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-taylor74_80-28">[28]</a></sup> With his teammates, Russell had a cordial relationship, with the notable exception of fellow rookie and old rival Heinsohn. Heinsohn felt that Russell resented him because the former was named the 1957 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBA_Rookie_of_the_Year" title="NBA Rookie of the Year">NBA Rookie of the Year</a>: many people thought that Russell was more important, but Russell also had only played half the season. Russell also ignored Heinsohn's plea to give his cousin an autograph, and openly said to Heinsohn that he deserved half of his $300 Rookie of the Year check. The relationship between the two rookies remained reserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor91_99_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor91_99-30">[30]</a></sup> On the other hand, despite their different ethnic backgrounds and lack of common off-court interests, his relationship with Celtics point guard and fan favorite Bob Cousy was amicable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor108_111_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor108_111-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1957_NBA_Playoffs" title="1957 NBA Playoffs">Game 1 of the Eastern Division Finals</a>, the Celtics met the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syracuse_Nationals" title="Syracuse Nationals">Syracuse Nationals</a>, who were led by <a href="/wiki/Dolph_Schayes" title="Dolph Schayes">Dolph Schayes</a>. In Russell's first NBA playoff game, he finished with 16 points and 31 rebounds, along with a reported 7 blocks. (At the time, blocks were not yet an officially registered statistic.) After the Celtics' 108–89 victory, Schayes quipped, "How much does that guy make a year? It would be to our advantage if we paid him off for five years to get away from us in the rest of this series."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup> The Celtics swept the Nationals in three games to earn the franchise's first appearance in <a href="/wiki/1957_NBA_Finals" title="1957 NBA Finals">the NBA Finals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the NBA Finals, the Celtics met the St. Louis Hawks, who were again led by Bob Pettit, as well as former Celtic Ed Macauley. The teams split the first six games, and the tension was so high that, in Game 3, Celtics coach Auerbach punched his colleague Ben Kerner and received a $300 fine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor91_99_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor91_99-30">[30]</a></sup> In the highly competitive Game 7, Russell tried his best to slow down Pettit, but it was Heinsohn who scored 37 points and kept the Celtics alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor91_99_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor91_99-30">[30]</a></sup> However, Russell contributed by completing the famous "Coleman Play". Here, Russell ran down Hawks guard <a href="/wiki/Jack_Coleman_(basketball)" title="Jack Coleman (basketball)">Jack Coleman</a>, who had received an outlet pass at midcourt, and blocked his shot despite the fact that Russell had been standing at his own baseline when the ball was thrown to Coleman. The block preserved Boston's slim 103–102 lead with 40-odd seconds left to play in regulation, saving the game for the Celtics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup> In the second <a href="/wiki/Overtime_(sports)" title="Overtime (sports)">overtime</a>, both teams were in serious foul trouble: Heinsohn had fouled out, and the Hawks were so depleted that they had only 7 players left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor91_99_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-taylor91_99-30">[30]</a></sup> With the Celtics leading 125–123 with one second left, the Hawks had the ball at their own baseline. Reserve guard <a href="/wiki/Alex_Hannum" title="Alex Hannum">Alex Hannum</a> threw a long <a href="/wiki/Alley-oop_(basketball)" title="Alley-oop (basketball)">alley oop</a> pass to Pettit, and Pettit's tip-in rolled indecisively on the rim for several seconds before rolling out again. The Celtics won, earning their first NBA Championship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor91_99_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-taylor91_99-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the start of the <a href="/wiki/1957%E2%80%9358_NBA_season" title="1957–58 NBA season">1957–58 season</a>, the Celtics won 14 straight games, and continued to succeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Russell averaged 16.6 points per game and a league-record average of 22.7 rebounds per game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> An interesting phenomenon began that year: Russell was voted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBA_Most_Valuable_Player" title="NBA Most Valuable Player">NBA Most Valuable Player</a>, but only named to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-NBA" title="All-NBA">All-NBA</a> Second Team. This would occur repeatedly throughout his career. The NBA reasoned that other centers were better all-round players than Russell, but no player was more valuable to his team. The Celtics won 49 games and easily made the first berth in the <a href="/wiki/1958_NBA_Playoffs" title="1958 NBA Playoffs">1958 NBA Playoffs</a>, and made the <a href="/wiki/1958_NBA_Finals" title="1958 NBA Finals">1958 NBA Finals</a> against their familiar rivals, the St. Louis Hawks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hawks58_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-hawks58-34">[34]</a></sup> The teams split the first two games, but then Russell went down with a foot injury in Game 3 and only returned for Game 6. The Celtics surprisingly won Game 4, but the Hawks prevailed in Games 5 and 6, with Pettit scoring 50 points in the deciding Game 6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hawks58_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-hawks58-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the following <a href="/wiki/1958%E2%80%9359_NBA_season" title="1958–59 NBA season">1958–59 season</a>, Russell continued his strong play, averaging 16.7 points per game and 23.0 rebounds per game in the regular season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> The Celtics broke a league record by winning 52 games, and Russell's strong performance once again helped lead the Celtics through the post-season, as they returned to the NBA Finals. In the <a href="/wiki/1959_NBA_Finals" title="1959 NBA Finals">1959 NBA Finals</a>, the Celtics recaptured the NBA title, sweeping the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minneapolis_Lakers" title="Minneapolis Lakers">Minneapolis Lakers</a> 4–0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Lakers head coach <a href="/wiki/John_Kundla" title="John Kundla">John Kundla</a> praised Russell, stating, "We don't fear the Celtics without Bill Russell. Take him out and we can beat them ... He's the guy who whipped us psychologically."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1959.E2.80.9366">1959–66</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1959–66">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/1959%E2%80%9360_NBA_season" title="1959–60 NBA season">1959–60 season</a>, the NBA witnessed the debut of legendary 7 ft 1 in (2.16 m) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Warriors" title="Philadelphia Warriors">Philadelphia Warriors</a> center <a href="/wiki/Wilt_Chamberlain" title="Wilt Chamberlain">Wilt Chamberlain</a>, who averaged a record 37.6 points per game in his rookie year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiltsummary_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-wiltsummary-36">[36]</a></sup> On November 7, 1959, Russell's Celtics hosted Chamberlain's Warriors, and pundits called the matchup between the best offensive and best defensive center "The Big Collision" and "Battle of the Titans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor3_10_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor3_10-37">[37]</a></sup> Both men awed onlookers with "nakedly awesome athleticism",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor3_10_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor3_10-37">[37]</a></sup> and while Chamberlain outscored Russell 30 to 22, the Celtics won 115–106, and the match was called a "new beginning of basketball".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor3_10_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor3_10-37">[37]</a></sup> The matchup between Russell and Chamberlain became one of basketball's greatest rivalries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> In that season, Russell's Celtics won a record 59 regular season games (including a then-record tying <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_longest_winning_streaks" title="List of National Basketball Association longest winning streaks">17 game win streak</a>) and met Chamberlain's Warriors <a href="/wiki/1960_NBA_Playoffs" title="1960 NBA Playoffs">in the Eastern Division Finals</a>. Chamberlain outscored Russell by 81 points in the series, but the Celtics walked off with a 4–2 series win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1960_NBA_Finals" title="1960 NBA Finals">1960 Finals</a>, the Celtics outlasted the Hawks 4–3 and won their third championship in four years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brceltics_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-brceltics-29">[29]</a></sup> Russell grabbed an NBA Finals-record 40 rebounds in Game 2, and added 22 points and 35 rebounds in the deciding Game 7, a 122–103 victory for Boston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/1960%E2%80%9361_NBA_season" title="1960–61 NBA season">1960–61 season</a>, Russell averaged 16.9 points and 23.9 rebounds per game,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> leading his team to a regular season mark of 57–22. The Celtics earned another post-season appearance, where they defeated the Syracuse Nationals 4–1 <a href="/wiki/1961_NBA_Playoffs" title="1961 NBA Playoffs">in the Eastern Division Finals</a>. The Celtics made good use of the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Lakers" title="Los Angeles Lakers">Los Angeles Lakers</a> had exhausted St. Louis in a long seven-game Western Conference Finals, and the Celtics convincingly won in five games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-celtics61_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-celtics61-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1961%E2%80%9362_NBA_season" title="1961–62 NBA season">following season</a>, Russell scored a career-high 18.9 points per game, accompanied by 23.6 rebounds per game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> While his rival Chamberlain had a record-breaking season of 50.4 points per game and a <a href="/wiki/Wilt_Chamberlain%27s_100-point_game" title="Wilt Chamberlain's 100-point game">100-point game</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiltsummary_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-wiltsummary-36">[36]</a></sup> the Celtics became the first team to win 60 games in a season, and Russell was voted as the NBA's Most Valuable Player. In the post-season, the Celtics met the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Warriors" title="Philadelphia Warriors">Philadelphia Warriors</a> of Chamberlain, and Russell did his best to slow down the 50-points-per-game scoring Warriors center. In the pivotal Game 7, Russell managed to hold Chamberlain to only 22 points (28 below his season average) while scoring 19 himself. The game was tied with two seconds left when <a href="/wiki/Sam_Jones_(basketball)" title="Sam Jones (basketball)">Sam Jones</a> sank a clutch shot that won the Celtics the series. In the <a href="/wiki/1962_NBA_Finals" title="1962 NBA Finals">1962 NBA Finals</a>, the Celtics met the Los Angeles Lakers of star forward <a href="/wiki/Elgin_Baylor" title="Elgin Baylor">Elgin Baylor</a> and star guard <a href="/wiki/Jerry_West" title="Jerry West">Jerry West</a>. The teams split the first six games, and Game 7 was tied one second before the end of regular time when Lakers guard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rod_Hundley" title="Rod Hundley">Rod Hundley</a> faked a shot and instead passed out to <a href="/wiki/Frank_Selvy" title="Frank Selvy">Frank Selvy</a>, who missed an open eight-foot last-second shot that would have won L.A. the title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor167_170_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor167_170-41">[41]</a></sup> Though the game was tied, Russell had the daunting task of defending against Baylor with little frontline help, as the three best Celtics forwards, Loscutoff, Heinsohn and <a href="/wiki/Satch_Sanders" title="Satch Sanders">Tom Sanders</a>, had fouled out. In overtime, Baylor fouled out the fourth forward, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Ramsey_(basketball)" title="Frank Ramsey (basketball)">Frank Ramsey</a>, so Russell was completely robbed of his usual four-men wing rotation. But Russell and little-used fifth forward <a href="/wiki/Gene_Guarilia" title="Gene Guarilia">Gene Guarilia</a> successfully pressured Baylor into missed shots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor167_170_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor167_170-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Russell finished with a <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(sports)" title="Clutch (sports)">clutch performance</a>, scoring 30 points and tying his own NBA Finals record with 40 rebounds in a 110–107 overtime win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Celtics lost playmaker Bob Cousy to retirement after the <a href="/wiki/1962%E2%80%9363_NBA_season" title="1962–63 NBA season">1962–63 season</a>, but they drafted <a href="/wiki/John_Havlicek" title="John Havlicek">John Havlicek</a>. Once again, the Celtics were powered by Russell, who averaged 16.8 points and 23.6 rebounds per game, won his fourth regular-season MVP title, and earned MVP honors at the <a href="/wiki/1963_NBA_All-Star_Game" title="1963 NBA All-Star Game">1963 NBA All-Star Game</a> following his 19-point, 24-rebound performance for the East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> The Celtics reached the <a href="/wiki/1963_NBA_Finals" title="1963 NBA Finals">1963 NBA Finals</a>, where they again defeated the Los Angeles Lakers, this time in six games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the following <a href="/wiki/1963%E2%80%9364_NBA_season" title="1963–64 NBA season">1963–64 season</a>, the Celtics posted a league-best 58–22 record in the regular season. Russell scored 15.0 ppg and grabbed a career-high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_top_individual_rebounding_season_averages" title="List of National Basketball Association top individual rebounding season averages">24.7 rebounds per game</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_annual_rebounding_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association annual rebounding leaders">leading the NBA in rebounds</a> for the first time since Chamberlain entered the league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> Boston defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Royals" title="Cincinnati Royals">Cincinnati Royals</a> 4–1 to earn another NBA Finals appearance, and then won against Chamberlain's newly relocated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Warriors" title="San Francisco Warriors">San Francisco Warriors</a> 4–1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> It was their sixth consecutive and seventh title in Russell's eighth year, a streak unreached in any U.S. professional sports league. Russell later called the Celtics' defense the best of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Russell again excelled during the <a href="/wiki/1964%E2%80%9365_NBA_season" title="1964–65 NBA season">1964–65 season</a>. The Celtics won a league-record 62 games, and Russell averaged 14.1 points and 24.1 rebounds per game, winning his second consecutive rebounding title and his fifth MVP award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-7"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1965_NBA_Playoffs" title="1965 NBA Playoffs">1965 NBA Playoffs</a>, the Celtics played the Eastern Division Finals against the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_76ers" title="Philadelphia 76ers">Philadelphia 76ers</a>, who had recently traded for Wilt Chamberlain. Russell held Chamberlain to a pair of field goals in the first three quarters of Game 3. In Game 5, Russell contributed 28 rebounds, 10 blocks, seven <a href="/wiki/Assist_(basketball)" title="Assist (basketball)">assists</a> and six <a href="/wiki/Steal_(basketball)" title="Steal (basketball)">steals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup> However, that playoff series ended in a dramatic Game 7. Five seconds before the end, the Sixers were trailing 110–109, but Russell turned over the ball. However, when the Sixers' Hall-of-Fame guard <a href="/wiki/Hal_Greer" title="Hal Greer">Hal Greer</a> inbounded, John Havlicek stole the ball, causing Celtics commentator <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Most" title="Johnny Most">Johnny Most</a> to scream: "Havlicek stole the ball! It's all over! Johnny Havlicek stole the ball!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> After the Division Finals, the Celtics had an easier time in the NBA Finals, winning 4–1 against the Los Angeles Lakers of Jerry West and Elgin Baylor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the following <a href="/wiki/1965%E2%80%9366_NBA_season" title="1965–66 NBA season">1965–66 season</a>, the Celtics won their <a href="/wiki/1966_NBA_Finals" title="1966 NBA Finals">eighth consecutive title</a>. Russell's team again beat Chamberlain's Philadelphia 76ers 4 games to 1 in the Division Finals, proceeding to win the NBA Finals in a tight seven-game showdown against the Los Angeles Lakers, with Russell scoring 25 points and grabbing 32 rebounds in a 95–93 win in the deciding seventh game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> During the season, Russell contributed 12.9 points and 22.8 rebounds per game. This was the first time in seven years that he failed to average at least 23 rebounds a game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-8"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1966.E2.80.9369">1966–69</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1966–69">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilt_Chamberlain_Bill_Russell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="909" data-file-width="670" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Wilt_Chamberlain_Bill_Russell.jpg/200px-Wilt_Chamberlain_Bill_Russell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Wilt_Chamberlain_Bill_Russell.jpg/300px-Wilt_Chamberlain_Bill_Russell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Wilt_Chamberlain_Bill_Russell.jpg/400px-Wilt_Chamberlain_Bill_Russell.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wilt_Chamberlain_Bill_Russell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Russell defending against <a href="/wiki/Wilt_Chamberlain" title="Wilt Chamberlain">Wilt Chamberlain</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_76ers" title="Philadelphia 76ers">Philadelphia 76ers</a> in 1966</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bill_Russell_and_Red_Auerbach_1966_Champions.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="756" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Bill_Russell_and_Red_Auerbach_1966_Champions.jpg/200px-Bill_Russell_and_Red_Auerbach_1966_Champions.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Bill_Russell_and_Red_Auerbach_1966_Champions.jpg/300px-Bill_Russell_and_Red_Auerbach_1966_Champions.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Bill_Russell_and_Red_Auerbach_1966_Champions.jpg/400px-Bill_Russell_and_Red_Auerbach_1966_Champions.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bill_Russell_and_Red_Auerbach_1966_Champions.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Russell and coach Red Auerbach after winning the 1966 NBA Finals</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/1966%E2%80%9367_NBA_season" title="1966–67 NBA season">1966–67 season</a>, Celtics coach <a href="/wiki/Red_Auerbach" title="Red Auerbach">Red Auerbach</a> retired. Initially, he had wanted his old player Frank Ramsey as coach, but Ramsey was too occupied running his three lucrative nursing homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor264_272_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor264_272-47">[47]</a></sup> His second choice Bob Cousy declined, stating he did not want to coach his former teammates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor264_272_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor264_272-47">[47]</a></sup> and the third choice Tom Heinsohn also said no, because he did not think he could handle the often surly Russell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor264_272_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor264_272-47">[47]</a></sup> However, Heinsohn proposed Russell himself as a player-coach, and when Auerbach asked his center, he said yes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor264_272_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-taylor264_272-47">[47]</a></sup> Russell thus became the first African American head coach in NBA history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> and commented to journalists: "I wasn't offered the job because I am a Negro, I was offered it because Red figured I could do it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor264_272_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-taylor264_272-47">[47]</a></sup> The Celtics' championship streak ended that season at eight, however, as Wilt Chamberlain's Philadelphia 76ers won a record-breaking 68 regular season games and overcame the Celtics 4–1 in the Eastern Finals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The Sixers simply outpaced the Celtics, shredding the famous Boston defense by scoring 140 points in the clinching Game 5 win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor292_299_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor292_299-49">[49]</a></sup> Russell acknowledged his first real loss in his career (he had been injured in 1958 when the Celtics lost the NBA Finals) by visiting Chamberlain in the locker room, shaking his hand and saying, "Great".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor292_299_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor292_299-49">[49]</a></sup> However, the game still ended on a high note for Russell. After the loss, he led his grandfather through the Celtics locker rooms, and the two saw white Celtics player <a href="/wiki/John_Havlicek" title="John Havlicek">John Havlicek</a> taking a shower next to his black teammate <a href="/wiki/Sam_Jones_(basketball)" title="Sam Jones (basketball)">Sam Jones</a> and discussing the game. Suddenly, Russell Sr. broke down crying. Asked by his grandson what was wrong, his grandfather replied how proud he was of him, being coach of an organization in which blacks and whites coexisted in harmony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor292_299_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor292_299-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Russell's penultimate season, the <a href="/wiki/1967%E2%80%9368_NBA_season" title="1967–68 NBA season">1967–68 season</a>, his numbers slowly declined, but at age 34, he still tallied 12.5 points per game and 18.6 rebounds per game<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-9"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> (the latter good for the third highest average in the league).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1968_NBA_Season_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-1968_NBA_Season-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1967_NBA_Playoffs" title="1967 NBA Playoffs">In the Eastern Division Finals</a>, the 76ers had the better record than the Celtics and were slightly favored. But then, national tragedy struck as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King" title="Martin Luther King">Martin Luther King</a> was assassinated on April 4, 1968. With eight of the ten starting players on Sixers and Celtics being African American, both teams were in deep shock, and there were calls to cancel the series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherry190_199_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-cherry190_199-51">[51]</a></sup> In a game called as "unreal" and "devoid of emotion", the Sixers lost 127–118 on April 5. In Game 2, Philadelphia evened the series with a 115–106 win, and in Games 3 and 4, the Sixers won, with Chamberlain suspiciously often defended by Celtics backup center <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Embry" title="Wayne Embry">Wayne Embry</a>, causing the press to speculate Russell was worn down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherry190_199_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-cherry190_199-51">[51]</a></sup> Prior to Game 5, the Celtics seemed dead: no NBA team had ever come back from a 3–1 deficit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherry190_199_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-cherry190_199-51">[51]</a></sup> However, the Celtics rallied back, winning Game 5 122–104 and Game 6 114–106, powered by a spirited Havlicek and helped by a terrible Sixers shooting slump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherry190_199_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-cherry190_199-51">[51]</a></sup> In Game 7, 15,202 stunned Philadelphia fans witnessed a historic 100–96 defeat, making it the first time in NBA history a team lost a series after leading 3–1. Russell limited Chamberlain to only two shot attempts in the second half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-7"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup> Despite this, the Celtics were leading only 97–95 with 34 seconds left when Russell closed out the game with several consecutive clutch plays. He made a free throw, blocked a shot by Sixers player <a href="/wiki/Chet_Walker" title="Chet Walker">Chet Walker</a>, grabbed a rebound off a miss by Sixers player Hal Greer, and finally passed the ball to teammate Sam Jones, who scored to clinch the win. Boston then beat the Los Angeles Lakers 4–2 in the <a href="/wiki/1968_NBA_Finals" title="1968 NBA Finals">NBA Finals</a>, giving Russell his tenth title in 12 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> For his efforts Russell was named <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a>'</i>s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sportsman_of_the_Year" title="Sportsman of the Year">Sportsman of the Year</a>. After losing for the fifth straight time against Russell and his Celtics, Hall-of-Fame Lakers guard Jerry West stated, "If I had a choice of any basketball player in the league, my No.1 choice has to be Bill Russell. Bill Russell never ceases to amaze me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-8"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, in the <a href="/wiki/1968%E2%80%9369_NBA_season" title="1968–69 NBA season">1968–69 season</a>, Russell seemed to reach a breaking point. Shocked by the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of Robert F. Kennedy">murder of Robert F. Kennedy</a>, disillusioned by the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, and weary from his increasingly stale (and later divorced) marriage to his wife Rose, he was convinced that the U.S. was a corrupt nation and that he was wasting his time playing something as superficial as basketball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor327_335_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor327_335-52">[52]</a></sup> He was 15 pounds overweight, skipped mandatory NBA coach meetings and was generally lacking energy: after a <a href="/wiki/New_York_Knicks" title="New York Knicks">New York Knicks</a> game, he complained of intense pain and was diagnosed with acute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(physical)" title="Fatigue (physical)">exhaustion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor327_335_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor327_335-52">[52]</a></sup> Russell pulled himself together and put up 9.9 points and 19.3 rebounds per game,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-10"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup> but the aging Celtics stumbled through the regular season. Their 48–34 record was the team's worst since <a href="/wiki/1955%E2%80%9356_NBA_season" title="1955–56 NBA season">1955–56</a>, and they entered <a href="/wiki/1969_NBA_Playoffs" title="1969 NBA Playoffs">the playoffs</a> as only the fourth-seeded team in the East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In the playoffs, however, Russell and his Celtics achieved upsets over the Philadelphia 76ers and New York Knicks to earn a meeting with the Los Angeles Lakers in <a href="/wiki/1969_NBA_Finals" title="1969 NBA Finals">the NBA Finals</a>. L.A. now featured new recruit Wilt Chamberlain next to perennial stars Baylor and West, and were heavily favored. In the first two games, Russell ordered not to double-team West, who used the freedom to score 53 and 41 points in the Game 1 and 2 Laker wins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor336_353_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor336_353-54">[54]</a></sup> Russell then ordered to double-team West, and Boston won Game 3. In Game 4, the Celtics were trailing by one point with seven seconds left and the Lakers having the ball, but then Baylor stepped out of bounds, and in the last play, Sam Jones used a triple screen by <a href="/wiki/Bailey_Howell" title="Bailey Howell">Bailey Howell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Siegfried" title="Larry Siegfried">Larry Siegfried</a> and Havlicek and hit a <a href="/wiki/Buzzer_beater" title="Buzzer beater">buzzer beater</a> which equalized the series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor336_353_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor336_353-54">[54]</a></sup> The teams split the next two games, so it all came down to Game 7 in L.A., where Lakers owner <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kent_Cooke" title="Jack Kent Cooke">Jack Kent Cooke</a> angered and motivated the Celtics by putting "proceedings of Lakers victory ceremony" on the game leaflets. Russell used a copy as extra motivation and told his team to play a running game, because in that case, not the better, but the more determined team was going to win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor336_353_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor336_353-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Celtics were ahead by nine points with five minutes remaining; in addition, West was heavily limping after a Game 5 thigh injury and Chamberlain had left the game with an injured leg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor336_353_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-taylor336_353-54">[54]</a></sup> West then hit one basket after the other and cut the lead to one, and Chamberlain asked to return to the game. However, Lakers coach <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_van_Breda_Kolff" title="Bill van Breda Kolff">Bill van Breda Kolff</a> kept Chamberlain on the bench until the end of the game, saying later that he wanted to stay with the lineup responsible for the comeback.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiltsummary_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-wiltsummary-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The Celtics held on for a 108–106 victory, and Russell claimed his eleventh championship in 13 years. At age 35, Russell contributed 21 rebounds in his last NBA game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-9"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup> After the game, Russell went over to the distraught West (who had scored 42 points and was named the only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBA_Finals_MVP" title="NBA Finals MVP">NBA Finals MVP</a> in history from the losing team), clasped his hand and tried to soothe him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor336_353_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-taylor336_353-54">[54]</a></sup> Days later, 30,000 enthusiastic Celtics fans cheered their returning heroes, but Russell was not there: the man who said he owed the public nothing ended his career and cut all ties to the Celtics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor336_353_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-taylor336_353-54">[54]</a></sup> It came as so surprising that even Red Auerbach was blindsided, and as a consequence, he made the "mistake" of drafting guard <a href="/wiki/Jo_Jo_White" title="Jo Jo White">Jo Jo White</a> instead of a center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor358_359_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor358_359-56">[56]</a></sup> Although White became a standout Celtics player, the Celtics lacked an All-Star center, went just 34–48 in the next season and failed to make the playoffs for the first time since 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brceltics_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-brceltics-29">[29]</a></sup> In Boston, both fans and journalists felt betrayed, because Russell left the Celtics without a coach and a center and sold his retirement story for $10,000 to <i>Sports Illustrated</i>. Russell was accused of selling out the future of the franchise for a month of his salary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor358_359_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor358_359-56">[56]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-playing_career">Post-playing career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Post-playing career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Russell's No. 6 jersey was retired by the Celtics on March 12, 1972,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Besides the Celtics, Russell also wore number 6 at the University of San Francisco and for the 1956 USA Olympic Team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Naismith_Memorial_Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame">Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame</a> in 1975. Russell, who had a difficult relationship with the media, was not present at either event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-espn_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-espn-59">[59]</a></sup> After retiring as a player, Russell had stints as head coach of the <a href="/wiki/Seattle_SuperSonics" title="Seattle SuperSonics">Seattle SuperSonics</a> (1973 to 1977) and <a href="/wiki/Sacramento_Kings" title="Sacramento Kings">Sacramento Kings</a> (1987 to 1988). His time as a coach was lackluster; although he led the struggling SuperSonics into the playoffs for the first time in franchise history, Russell's defensive, team-oriented Celtics mindset did not mesh well with the team, and he left in 1977 with a 162–166 record. Russell's stint with the Kings was considerably shorter, his last assignment ending when the Kings went 17–41 to begin the <a href="/wiki/1987%E2%80%9388_NBA_season" title="1987–88 NBA season">1987–88 season</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, Russell ran into financial trouble. He had invested $250,000 into a rubber plantation in <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, where he had wanted to spend his retirement, but it went bankrupt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> The same fate awaited his Boston restaurant called "Slade's", after which he had to default on a $90,000 government loan to purchase the outlet. The <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">IRS</a> discovered that Russell owed $34,430 in tax money and put a lien on his house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor359_366_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor359_366-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Russell became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">vegetarian</a>, took up <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a> and worked as a <a href="/wiki/Color_commentator" title="Color commentator">color commentator</a>, but he was uncomfortable as a broadcaster. He later said, "The most successful television is done in eight-second thoughts, and the things I know about basketball, motivation, and people go deeper than that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor359_366_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor359_366-62">[62]</a></sup> On November 3, 1979, Russell hosted <a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Live" title="Saturday Night Live">Saturday Night Live</a>, in which he appeared in several sports-related sketches. Russell also wrote books, usually written as a joint project with a professional writer, including 1979's <i>Second Wind</i>.</p>
<p>After spending about a decade living as a recluse on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercer_Island" title="Mercer Island">Mercer Island</a> near <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, Russell rose to prominence again at the turn of the millennium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <i>Russell's Rules</i> was published in 2001, and in January 2006, he convinced <a href="/wiki/Miami_Heat" title="Miami Heat">Miami Heat</a> superstar center <a href="/wiki/Shaquille_O%27Neal" title="Shaquille O'Neal">Shaquille O'Neal</a> to bury the hatchet with fellow NBA superstar and former Los Angeles Lakers teammate <a href="/wiki/Kobe_Bryant" title="Kobe Bryant">Kobe Bryant</a>, with whom O'Neal had a <a href="/wiki/Shaq%E2%80%93Kobe_feud" title="Shaq–Kobe feud">bitter public feud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Later that year, on November 17, 2006, the two-time NCAA winner Russell was recognized for his impact on college basketball as a member of the founding class of the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="National Collegiate Basketball Hall of Fame">National Collegiate Basketball Hall of Fame</a>. He was one of five, along with <a href="/wiki/John_Wooden" title="John Wooden">John Wooden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Robertson" title="Oscar Robertson">Oscar Robertson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dean_Smith" title="Dean Smith">Dean Smith</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dr._James_Naismith" title="Dr. James Naismith">Dr. James Naismith</a>, selected to represent the inaugural class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> On May 20, 2007, Russell was awarded an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honorary_doctorate" title="Honorary doctorate">honorary doctorate</a> by <a href="/wiki/Suffolk_University" title="Suffolk University">Suffolk University</a>, where he served as its commencement speaker, and Russell received an honorary degree from <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> on June 7, 2007. On June 18, 2007, Russell was inducted as a member of the founding class of the <a href="/wiki/FIBA_Hall_of_Fame" title="FIBA Hall of Fame">FIBA Hall of Fame</a>. Russell was also honored during the <a href="/wiki/2009_NBA_All-Star_Game" title="2009 NBA All-Star Game">2009 NBA All-Star Weekend</a> in <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix</a>.</p>
<p>On February 14, 2009, NBA Commissioner <a href="/wiki/David_Stern" title="David Stern">David Stern</a> announced that the NBA Finals Most Valuable Player Award would be renamed the "<a href="/wiki/Bill_Russell_NBA_Finals_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Bill Russell NBA Finals Most Valuable Player Award">Bill Russell NBA Finals Most Valuable Player Award</a>" in honor of the 11-time NBA champion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalsmvp_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-finalsmvp-4">[4]</a></sup> The following day, during halftime of the All-Star game, Celtics captains <a href="/wiki/Paul_Pierce" title="Paul Pierce">Paul Pierce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Garnett" title="Kevin Garnett">Kevin Garnett</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ray_Allen" title="Ray Allen">Ray Allen</a> presented Russell a surprise birthday cake for his 75th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Russell attended the final game of the <a href="/wiki/2009_NBA_Finals" title="2009 NBA Finals">Finals that year</a> to present his newly christened namesake award to its winner, <a href="/wiki/Kobe_Bryant" title="Kobe Bryant">Kobe Bryant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Russell was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
