<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accomplishments_and_legacy">Accomplishments and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Accomplishments and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DIG13619-512.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4488" data-file-width="6732" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/DIG13619-512.jpg/220px-DIG13619-512.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/DIG13619-512.jpg/330px-DIG13619-512.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/DIG13619-512.jpg/440px-DIG13619-512.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DIG13619-512.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Former President Bill Clinton and Russell at the Civil Rights Summit at the LBJ Presidential Library in 2014</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Russell is one of the most successful and decorated athletes in North American sports history. His awards and achievements include <a href="/wiki/List_of_NBA_players_with_most_championships" title="List of NBA players with most championships">11 NBA championships</a> as a player with the Boston Celtics in 13 seasons (including two NBA championships as <a href="/wiki/Player-coach" title="Player-coach">player/head coach</a>), and he is credited with having raised defensive play in the NBA to a new level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> By winning the 1956 NCAA Championship with USF and the 1957 NBA title with the Celtics, Russell became the first of only four players in basketball history to win an NCAA championship and an NBA Championship back-to-back (the others being <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bibby" title="Henry Bibby">Henry Bibby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magic_Johnson" title="Magic Johnson">Magic Johnson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Billy_Thompson_(basketball)" title="Billy Thompson (basketball)">Billy Thompson</a>). He also won two state championships in high school. In the interim, Russell collected an Olympic gold medal in 1956. His stint as coach of the Celtics was also of historical significance, as he became the first black head coach in major U.S. professional sports when he succeeded Red Auerbach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his first NBA full season (1957–58), Russell became the first player in NBA history to average more than 20 rebounds per game for an entire season, a feat he accomplished 10 times in his 13 seasons. Russell's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_players_with_most_rebounds_in_a_game" title="List of National Basketball Association players with most rebounds in a game">51 rebounds in a single game</a> is the second-highest performance ever, only trailing Chamberlain's all-time record of 55. He still holds the NBA record for rebounds in one half with 32 (vs. Philadelphia, on November 16, 1957). Career-wise in rebounds, Russell ranks second only to Wilt Chamberlain in <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_career_rebounding_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association career rebounding leaders">regular season total</a> (21,620) and average per game (22.5), and he led the NBA in average rebounds per game four times. Russell is the all-time playoff leader in total (4,104) and average (24.9) rebounds per game, he grabbed 40 rebounds in three separate playoff games (twice in the NBA Finals), and he never failed to average at least 20 rebounds per game in any of his 13 post-season campaigns. Russell also had seven regular season games with 40 or more rebounds, the NBA Finals record for highest rebound per game average (29.5 rpg, 1959) and by a rookie (22.9 rpg, 1957). In addition, Russell holds the <a href="/wiki/NBA_Finals" title="NBA Finals">NBA Finals</a> single-game record for most rebounds (40, March 29, 1960, vs. St. Louis, and April 18, 1962, vs. Los Angeles), most rebounds in a quarter (19, April 18, 1962 vs. Los Angeles), and most consecutive games with 20 or more rebounds (15 from April 9, 1960 – April 16, 1963).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stats_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-stats-72">[72]</a></sup> He also had 51 in one game, 49 in two others, and twelve straight seasons of 1,000 or more rebounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> Russell was known as one of the most <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(sports)" title="Clutch (sports)">clutch</a> players in the NBA. He played in 11 deciding games (10 times in Game 7s, once in a Game 5), and ended with a flawless 11–0 record. In these eleven games, Russell averaged 18 points and 29.45 rebounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-10"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the hardwood, he was considered the consummate defensive center, noted for his unmatched defensive intensity, his stellar basketball <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_quotient" title="Intelligence quotient">IQ</a> and his sheer will to win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-11"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup> Russell excelled at playing man-to-man defense, blocking shots, and grabbing defensive and offensive rebounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> He also could score with putbacks and made mid-air outlet passes to point guard Bob Cousy for easy fast break points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> He also was known as a fine passer and <a href="/wiki/Pick_and_roll" title="Pick and roll">pick-setter</a>, featured a decent left-handed <a href="/wiki/Hook_shot" title="Hook shot">hook shot</a> and finished strong on alley oops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacompraise_22-12"><a href="#cite_note-nbacompraise-22">[22]</a></sup> However, on offense, Russell's output was limited. His NBA career personal averages show him to be an average scorer (15.1 points career average), a poor <a href="/wiki/Free_throw" title="Free throw">free throw</a> shooter (56.1%), and average overall shooter from the field (44%, not exceptional for a center). In his 13 years, he averaged a relatively low 13.4 field goals attempted (normally, top scorers average 20 and more), illustrating that he was never the focal point of the Celtics offense, instead focusing on his tremendous defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brstats_26-11"><a href="#cite_note-brstats-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his career, Russell won five regular season MVP awards (1959, 1961–63, 1965)—tied with <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan" title="Michael Jordan">Michael Jordan</a> for second all-time behind <a href="/wiki/Kareem_Abdul-Jabbar" title="Kareem Abdul-Jabbar">Kareem Abdul-Jabbar</a>'s six awards. He was selected three times to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-NBA_First_Team" title="All-NBA First Team">All-NBA First Teams</a> (1959, 1963, 1965) and eight Second Teams (1958, 1960–62, 1964, 1966–68), and was a twelve-time NBA All-Star (1958–1969). Russell was elected to one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBA_All-Defensive_First_Team" title="NBA All-Defensive First Team">NBA All-Defensive First Team</a>. This took place during his last season (1969), and was the first season the NBA All-Defensive Teams were selected. In 1970, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Sporting_News" title="The Sporting News">The Sporting News</a></i> named Russell the "Athlete of the Decade". Russell is universally seen as one of the best NBA players ever,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> and was declared "Greatest Player in the History of the NBA" by the Professional Basketball Writers Association of America in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbacomsummary_1-16"><a href="#cite_note-nbacomsummary-1">[1]</a></sup> For his achievements, Russell was named "Sportsman of the Year" by <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i> in 1968. He also made all three NBA Anniversary Teams: the NBA 25th Anniversary All-Time Team (1970), the NBA 35th Anniversary All-Time Team (1980) and the NBA 50th Anniversary All-Time Team (1996). Russell ranked #18 on <i><a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>'s 50 Greatest Athletes of the 20th Century</i> in 1999. In 2009, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SLAM_Magazine" title="SLAM Magazine">SLAM Magazine</a></i> named Russell the #3 player of all time behind Michael Jordan and Wilt Chamberlain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Former NBA player and head coach, Don Nelson, described Bill Russell in a quote that says, "There are two types of superstars. One makes himself look good at the expense of the other guys on the floor. But there's another type who makes the players around him look better than they are, and that's the type Russell was."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>On Saturday, February 14, 2009, during the 2009 <a href="/wiki/NBA_All-Star_Weekend" title="NBA All-Star Weekend">NBA All-Star Weekend</a> in Phoenix, NBA Commissioner <a href="/wiki/David_Stern" title="David Stern">David Stern</a> announced that the <a href="/wiki/NBA_Finals" title="NBA Finals">NBA Finals</a> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Russell_NBA_Finals_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Bill Russell NBA Finals Most Valuable Player Award">MVP Award</a> would be named after Bill Russell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalsmvp_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-finalsmvp-4">[4]</a></sup> Russell was named as a 2010 recipient of the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bill_Russell_in_the_Green_Room.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="661" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Bill_Russell_in_the_Green_Room.jpg/170px-Bill_Russell_in_the_Green_Room.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Bill_Russell_in_the_Green_Room.jpg/255px-Bill_Russell_in_the_Green_Room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Bill_Russell_in_the_Green_Room.jpg/340px-Bill_Russell_in_the_Green_Room.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bill_Russell_in_the_Green_Room.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Russell at the White House in February 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Russell was married to his college sweetheart Rose Swisher from 1956 to 1973. They had three children, namely daughter <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Karen_Russell_(lawyer)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Karen Russell (lawyer) (page does not exist)">Karen Russell</a>, the television pundit and lawyer, and sons William Jr. and Jacob. However, the couple grew emotionally distant and divorced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor359_362_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor359_362-76">[76]</a></sup> In 1977, he married <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Anstett" title="Dorothy Anstett">Dorothy Anstett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miss_USA" title="Miss USA">Miss USA</a> of 1968,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor359_362_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor359_362-76">[76]</a></sup> but they divorced in 1980. The relationship was shrouded in controversy because Didi was white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In 1996, Russell married his third wife, Marilyn Nault;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> their marriage lasted until her death in January 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> He has been a resident of <a href="/wiki/Mercer_Island,_Washington" title="Mercer Island, Washington">Mercer Island, Washington</a> for over four decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> His older brother was the noted playwright <a href="/wiki/Charlie_L._Russell" title="Charlie L. Russell">Charlie L. Russell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1959, Bill Russell became the first NBA player to visit Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Russell is a member of <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Alpha_Psi" title="Kappa Alpha Psi">Kappa Alpha Psi</a> Fraternity, having been initiated into its Gamma Alpha chapter while a student at University of San Francisco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> On October 16, 2013, Russell was arrested for bringing a loaded .38-caliber <a href="/wiki/Smith_%26_Wesson" title="Smith &amp; Wesson">Smith &amp; Wesson</a> handgun to the <a href="/wiki/Seattle%E2%80%93Tacoma_International_Airport" title="Seattle–Tacoma International Airport">Seattle–Tacoma International Airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a Thursday evening, July 17, 2014 <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> (AP) news story, it was stated that,: "Boston Celtics officials say NBA Hall of Famer Bill Russell is doing OK after collapsing during a speaking engagement near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tahoe" title="Lake Tahoe">Lake Tahoe</a>. Team spokesman <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Olive&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Brian Olive (page does not exist)">Brian Olive</a> says the 80-year-old Russell felt faint after the Thursday morning fall at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyatt_Regency" title="Hyatt Regency">Hyatt Regency</a> <a href="/wiki/Resort" title="Resort">resort</a> in <a href="/wiki/Incline_Village,_Nevada" title="Incline Village, Nevada">Incline Village, Nevada</a>, but was planning on returning home to <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a> this evening. ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Earnings">Earnings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Earnings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During his career, Russell was one of the first big earners in NBA basketball. His 1956 rookie contract was worth $24,000, only fractionally smaller than the $25,000 of top earner <a href="/wiki/Bob_Cousy" title="Bob Cousy">Bob Cousy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor74_80_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-taylor74_80-28">[28]</a></sup> In contrast to other Celtics, who had to work in the offseason to maintain their standard of living (Heinsohn sold insurance, Gene Guarilia was a professional guitar player, Cousy ran a basketball camp, and Auerbach invested in plastics and a Chinese restaurant),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor174_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor174-86">[86]</a></sup> Russell never had to work part-time. When <a href="/wiki/Wilt_Chamberlain" title="Wilt Chamberlain">Wilt Chamberlain</a> became the first NBA player to earn $100,000 in salary in 1965, Russell went to Auerbach and demanded a $100,001 salary, which he promptly received.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor258_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor258-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Personality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Russell was driven by "a neurotic need to win", as his teammate Heinsohn observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> He was so tense before every game that he regularly threw up in the locker rooms; it happened so frequently that his fellow Celtics were more worried when it did <i>not</i> happen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor6_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor6-88">[88]</a></sup> He was also known for his natural authority. When he became player-coach in 1966, Russell bluntly said to his teammates that "he intended to cut all personal ties to other players", and seamlessly made the transition from their peer to their superior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor280_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor280-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>To teammates and friends, Russell was open and amicable, but was extremely distrusting and cold towards anyone else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> Journalists were often treated to the "Russell Glower", described as an "icily contemptuous stare accompanied by a long silence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> Russell was also notorious for his refusal to give autographs or even acknowledge the Celtics fans, so far that he was called "the most selfish, surly and uncooperative athlete" by one pundit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Russell.E2.80.93Chamberlain_relations">Russell–Chamberlain relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Russell–Chamberlain relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For most of his career, Russell was close friends with his perennial opponent <a href="/wiki/Wilt_Chamberlain" title="Wilt Chamberlain">Wilt Chamberlain</a>. Chamberlain often invited Russell over for <a href="/wiki/Thanksgiving_(United_States)" title="Thanksgiving (United States)">Thanksgiving</a> dinner, and at Russell's place, conversation mostly concerned Russell's electric trains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherry360f_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-cherry360f-90">[90]</a></sup> However, the close relationship ended after Game 7 of the <a href="/wiki/1969_NBA_Finals" title="1969 NBA Finals">1969 NBA Finals</a>, during which Chamberlain injured his knee with six minutes left and left the game. During a conversation with students, a reporter—unknown to Russell— heard Russell describe Chamberlain as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malingerer" title="Malingerer">malingerer</a> and accuse him of "copping out" of the game when it seemed that the Lakers would lose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor356_357_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor356_357-91">[91]</a></sup> Chamberlain was livid with Russell and saw him as a backstabber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor356_357_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor356_357-91">[91]</a></sup> Chamberlain's knee was injured so badly that he could not play the entire offseason and he ruptured it the next season. The two men did not talk to each other for over 20 years until Russell met with Chamberlain personally and apologized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor367_371_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor367_371-92">[92]</a></sup> When Chamberlain died in 1999, Chamberlain's nephew said that Russell was the second person he was told to call.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chat_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-chat-7">[7]</a></sup> At Chamberlain's eulogy, Russell stated that he did not consider them to be rivals, but rather to have a competition, and that the pair would "be friends through eternity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Racist_abuse.2C_controversy.2C_and_relationship_with_Boston_fans">Racist abuse, controversy, and relationship with Boston fans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Racist abuse, controversy, and relationship with Boston fans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Russell's life was marked by an uphill battle against <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> and controversial actions and statements in response to perceived racism. As a child, the young Russell witnessed how his parents were victims of racial abuse, and the family eventually moved into government <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Housing_project" title="Housing project">housing projects</a> to escape the daily torrent of bigotry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Current_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Current-5">[5]</a></sup> When he later became a standout amateur basketball player at USF, Russell recalled how he and his few fellow black colleagues were jeered by white students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jwr_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-jwr-15">[15]</a></sup> Even after he became a star on the Boston Celtics, Russell was the victim of racial abuse. When the NBA All-Stars toured the U.S. in the 1958 offseason, white hotel owners in segregated <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a> denied rooms to Russell and his black teammates, causing him to later write in his memoir <i>Go Up for Glory</i>, "It stood out, a wall which understanding cannot penetrate. You are a Negro. You are less. It covered every area. A living, smarting, hurting, smelling, greasy substance which covered you. A morass to fight from."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor108_111_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor108_111-31">[31]</a></sup> Before the 1961–62 season, Russell's team was scheduled to play in an exhibition game in <a href="/wiki/Lexington,_Kentucky" title="Lexington, Kentucky">Lexington, Kentucky</a>, when Russell and his black teammates were refused service at a local restaurant. He and the other black teammates refused to play in the exhibition game and flew home, drawing a great deal of controversy and publicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-espn_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-espn-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a consequence, Russell was extremely sensitive to all racial prejudice: according to Taylor, he often perceived insults even if others did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor66_71_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor66_71-21">[21]</a></sup> He was active in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Power" title="Black Power">Black Power</a> movement and supported <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a>'s decision to refuse to be drafted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> He was often called "Felton X", presumably in the tradition of the Nation of Islam's practice of replacing a European <a href="/wiki/Slave_name" title="Slave name">slave name</a> with an "X", and even purchased land in Liberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-5"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> Russell's public statements became increasingly militant, so far that he was quoted in a 1963 <i>Sports Illustrated</i> interview with the words: "I dislike most white people because they are people ... I like most blacks because I am black".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-6"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> However, when his white Celtics teammate Frank Ramsey asked whether he hated him, Russell claimed to be misquoted, but few believed it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-7"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> According to Taylor, Russell overlooked the fact that his career was only made possible by the white people who were proven anti-racists, namely his white high school coach George Powles (the person who encouraged him to play basketball), his white college coach Phil Woolpert (who integrated USF basketball), white Celtics coach Red Auerbach (who is <a href="/wiki/Red_Auerbach#No_color_barrier" title="Red Auerbach">regarded as an anti-racist pioneer</a> and made him the first black NBA coach), and white Celtics owner <a href="/wiki/Walter_A._Brown" title="Walter A. Brown">Walter A. Brown</a>, who gave him a high $24,000 rookie contract, just $1,000 shy of the top earning veteran Bob Cousy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor359_366_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor359_366-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of repeated racial bigotry, Russell refused to respond to fan acclaim or friendship from his neighbors, thinking it was insincere and hypocritical. This attitude contributed to his legendary bad rapport with fans and journalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor108_111_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor108_111-31">[31]</a></sup> He alienated Celtics fans by saying, "You owe the public the same it owes you, nothing! I refuse to smile and be nice to the kiddies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-8"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> This supported the opinion of many white fans that Russell (who was the highest-paid Celtic) was egotistical, paranoid and hypocritical. The already hostile atmosphere between Russell and Boston hit its apex when vandals broke into his house, covered the walls with racist graffiti, damaged his trophies and defecated in the beds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-9"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> In response, Russell described <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> as a "flea market of <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In <i>King Of The Court</i> by Aram Goudsouzian, he was quoted saying, "From my very first year I thought of myself as playing for the Celtics, not for Boston. The fans could do or think whatever they wanted."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> After his retirement, he described the Boston press as corrupt and racist; in response, Boston sports journalist Larry Claflin claimed that Russell himself was the real racist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor361_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor361-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The FBI maintained a file on Russell; this lends credibility to his observations that racism was an active force against him. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI">FBI</a> described Russell in his file as "an arrogant Negro who won't sign autographs for white children".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor193_197_61-10"><a href="#cite_note-taylor193_197-61">[61]</a></sup> This clearly denotes a hostile attitude, and gives insight into the nature of Russell's public persona, which was often perceived by the mostly-white media as overly harsh.</p>
<p>Russell refused to attend the ceremony when his #6 jersey was retired in 1972, or his induction into the Hall of Fame in 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-espn_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-espn-59">[59]</a></sup> While Russell still has sore feelings towards the city, there has been something of a reconciliation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macquarrie_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie-98">[98]</a></sup> and he has even visited the city regularly in recent years, something he never did in the years immediately after his retirement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macquarrie_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie-98">[98]</a></sup> When Russell originally retired, he demanded that his jersey be retired in an empty <a href="/wiki/Boston_Garden" title="Boston Garden">Boston Garden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandomir_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandomir-99">[99]</a></sup> In 1995, the Celtics left Boston Garden and entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FleetCenter_(Boston)" title="FleetCenter (Boston)">FleetCenter</a>, now the TD Garden, and as the main festive act, the Boston organization wanted to re-retire Russell's jersey in front of a sellout audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor359_366_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-taylor359_366-62">[62]</a></sup> Perennially wary of what he long perceived as the racist city of Boston, Russell decided to make amends and gave his approval. On May 6, 1999, the Celtics re-retired Russell's jersey in a ceremony attended by his on-court rival (and friend) Chamberlain, along with Celtics legend <a href="/wiki/Larry_Bird" title="Larry Bird">Larry Bird</a> and Hall of Famer Kareem Abdul-Jabbar. The crowd gave Russell a prolonged standing ovation, which brought tears to his eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandomir_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sandomir-99">[99]</a></sup> He thanked Chamberlain for taking him to the limit and "making [him] a better player" and the crowd for "allowing [him] to be a part of their lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor359_366_62-4"><a href="#cite_note-taylor359_366-62">[62]</a></sup> In December 2008, the We Are Boston Leadership Award was presented to Russell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statue_honoring_Russell">Statue honoring Russell</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Statue honoring Russell">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2012, the city and the Celtics announced that a statue honoring Russell would be placed on Boston's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_Hall_Plaza_(Boston)" title="City Hall Plaza (Boston)">City Hall Plaza</a>. The design features a statue of Russell in game action with 11 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plinths" title="Plinths">plinths</a> representing the 11 championships he helped the team win. The plinths will feature a key word and a related quote to illustrate Russell's multiple accomplishments. The Bill Russell Legacy Foundation, established by the Boston Celtics Shamrock Foundation, funded the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Bill Russell Legacy Project artwork by Ann Hirsch of <a href="/wiki/Somerville,_Massachusetts" title="Somerville, Massachusetts">Somerville, Massachusetts</a>, in collaboration with Pressley Associates Landscape Architects of <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, was unveiled on November 1, 2013, with Russell in attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>

<p><i>Pound sign (#) denotes interim head coach.</i></p>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
